A BILL 
To expand Americans’ access to the ballot box, reduce the 
influence of big money in politics, strengthen ethics rules 
for public servants, and implement other anti-corruption 
measures for the purpose of fortifying our democracy, 
and for other purposes. 
Be it enacted by the Senate and House of Representa-
1
tives of the United States of America in Congress assembled,

SEC. 3. FINDINGS OF GENERAL CONSTITUTIONAL AUTHOR-
1
ITY. 
2
Congress finds that the Constitution of the United 
3
States grants explicit and broad authority to protect the 
4
right to vote, to regulate elections for Federal office, and 
5
to defend the Nation’s democratic process. Congress en-
6
acts the ‘‘For the People Act of 2021’’ pursuant to this 
7
broad authority, including but not limited to the following: 
8
(1) Congress finds that it has broad authority 
9
to regulate the time, place, and manner of congres-
10
sional elections under the Elections Clause of the 
11
Constitution, article I, section 4, clause 1. The Su-
12
preme Court has affirmed that the ‘‘substantive 
13
scope’’ of the Elections Clause is ‘‘broad’’; that 
14
‘‘Times, Places, and Manner’’ are ‘‘comprehensive 
15
words which embrace authority to provide for a com-
16
plete code for congressional elections’’; and ‘‘[t]he 
17
power of Congress over the Times, Places and Man-
18
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
17 
•HR 1 IH
ner of congressional elections is paramount, and may 
1
be exercised at any time, and to any extent which 
2
it deems expedient; and so far as it is exercised, and 
3
no farther, the regulations effected supersede those 
4
of the State which are inconsistent therewith’’. Ari-
5
zona v. Inter Tribal Council of Arizona, 570 U.S. 1, 
6
8–9 (2013) (internal quotation marks and citations 
7
omitted). Indeed, ‘‘Congress has plenary and para-
8
mount jurisdiction over the whole subject’’ of con-
9
gressional elections, Ex parte Siebold, 100 U.S. (10 
10
Otto) 371, 388 (1879), and this power ‘‘may be ex-
11
ercised as and when Congress sees fit’’, and ‘‘so far 
12
as it extends and conflicts with the regulations of 
13
the State, necessarily supersedes them’’. Id. At 384. 
14
Among other things, Congress finds that the Elec-
15
tions Clause was intended to ‘‘vindicate the people’s 
16
right to equality of representation in the House’’. 
17
Wesberry v. Sanders, 376 U.S. 1, 16 (1964). 
18
(2) Congress also finds that it has both the au-
19
thority and responsibility, as the legislative body for 
20
the United States, to fulfill the promise of article IV, 
21
section 4, of the Constitution, which states: ‘‘The 
22
United States shall guarantee to every State in this 
23
Union a Republican Form of Government[.]’’. Con-
24
gress finds that its authority and responsibility to 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
18 
•HR 1 IH
enforce the Guarantee Clause is particularly strong 
1
given that Federal courts have not enforced this 
2
clause because they understood that its enforcement 
3
is committed to Congress by the Constitution. 
4
(3)(A) Congress also finds that it has broad au-
5
thority pursuant to section 5 of the Fourteenth 
6
Amendment to legislate to enforce the provisions of 
7
the Fourteenth Amendment, including its protec-
8
tions of the right to vote and the democratic process. 
9
(B) Section 1 of the Fourteenth Amendment 
10
protects the fundamental right to vote, which is ‘‘of 
11
the most fundamental significance under our con-
12
stitutional structure’’. Ill. Bd. of Election v. Socialist 
13
Workers Party, 440 U.S. 173, 184 (1979); see 
14
United States v. Classic, 313 U.S. 299 (1941) (‘‘Ob-
15
viously included within the right to choose, secured 
16
by the Constitution, is the right of qualified voters 
17
within a state to cast their ballots and have them 
18
counted . . .’’). As the Supreme Court has repeatedly 
19
affirmed, the right to vote is ‘‘preservative of all 
20
rights’’, Yick Wo v. Hopkins, 118 U.S. 356, 370 
21
(1886). Section 2 of the Fourteenth Amendment 
22
also protects the right to vote, granting Congress 
23
additional authority to reduce a State’s representa-
24
tion in Congress when the right to vote is denied. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
19 
•HR 1 IH
(C) As a result, Congress finds that it has the 
1
authority pursuant to section 5 of the Fourteenth 
2
Amendment to protect the right to vote. Congress 
3
also finds that States and localities have eroded ac-
4
cess to the right to vote through restrictions on the 
5
right to vote including excessively onerous voter 
6
identification requirements, burdensome voter reg-
7
istration procedures, voter purges, limited and un-
8
equal access to voting by mail, polling place closures, 
9
unequal distribution of election resources, and other 
10
impediments. 
11
(D) Congress also finds that ‘‘the right of suf-
12
frage can be denied by a debasement or dilution of 
13
the weight of a citizen’s vote just as effectively as by 
14
wholly prohibiting the free exercise of the franchise’’. 
15
Reynolds v. Sims, 377 U.S. 533, 555 (1964). Con-
16
gress finds that the right of suffrage has been so di-
17
luted and debased by means of gerrymandering of 
18
districts. Congress finds that it has authority pursu-
19
ant to section 5 of the Fourteenth Amendment to 
20
remedy this debasement. 
21
(4)(A) Congress also finds that it has authority 
22
to legislate to eliminate racial discrimination in vot-
23
ing and the democratic process pursuant to both sec-
24
tion 5 of the Fourteenth Amendment, which grants 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
20 
•HR 1 IH
equal protection of the laws, and section 2 of the 
1
Fifteenth Amendment, which explicitly bars denial 
2
or abridgment of the right to vote on account of 
3
race, color, or previous condition of servitude. 
4
(B) Congress finds that racial discrimination in 
5
access to voting and the political process persists. 
6
Voting restrictions, redistricting, and other electoral 
7
practices and processes continue to disproportion-
8
ately impact communities of color in the United 
9
States and do so as a result of both intentional ra-
10
cial discrimination, structural racism, and the ongo-
11
ing structural socioeconomic effects of historical ra-
12
cial discrimination. 
13
(C) Recent elections and studies have shown 
14
that minority communities wait longer in lines to 
15
vote, are more likely to have their mail ballots re-
16
jected, continue to face intimidation at the polls, are 
17
more likely to be disenfranchised by voter purges, 
18
and are disproportionately burdened by voter identi-
19
fication and other voter restrictions. Research shows 
20
that communities of color are more likely to face 
21
nearly every barrier to voting than their white coun-
22
terparts. 
23
(D) Congress finds that racial disparities in dis-
24
enfranchisement due to past felony convictions is 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
21 
•HR 1 IH
particularly stark. In 2020, according to the Sen-
1
tencing Project, an estimated 5,200,000 Americans 
2
could not vote due to a felony conviction. One in 16 
3
African Americans of voting age is disenfranchised, 
4
a rate 3.7 times greater than that of non-African 
5
Americans. In seven States–Alabama, Florida, Ken-
6
tucky, Mississippi, Tennessee, Virginia, and Wyo-
7
ming–more than one in seven African Americans is 
8
disenfranchised, twice the national average for Afri-
9
can Americans. Congress finds that felony disenfran-
10
chisement was one of the tools of intentional racial 
11
discrimination during the Jim Crow era. Congress 
12
further finds that current racial disparities in felony 
13
disenfranchisement are linked to this history of voter 
14
suppression, structural racism in the criminal justice 
15
system, and ongoing effects of historical discrimina-
16
tion. 
17
(5)(A) Congress finds that it further has the 
18
power to protect the right to vote from denial or 
19
abridgment on account of sex, age, or ability to pay 
20
a poll tax or other tax pursuant to the Nineteenth, 
21
Twenty-Fourth, and Twenty-Sixth Amendments. 
22
(B) Congress finds that electoral practices in-
23
cluding voting rights restoration conditions for peo-
24
ple with convictions, voter identification require-
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
22 
•HR 1 IH
ments, and other restrictions to the franchise burden 
1
voters on account of their ability to pay. 
2
(C) Congress further finds that electoral prac-
3
tices including voting restrictions related to college 
4
campuses, age restrictions on mail voting, and simi-
5
lar practices burden the right to vote on account of 
6
age. 
7
SEC. 4. STANDARDS FOR JUDICIAL REVIEW. 
8
(a) IN GENERAL.—If any action is brought for de-
9
claratory or injunctive relief to challenge, whether facially 
10
or as-applied, the constitutionality of any provision of this 
11
Act or any amendment made by this Act or any rule or 
12
regulation promulgated under this Act, the following rules 
13
shall apply: 
14
(1) The action shall be filed in the United 
15
States District Court for the District of Columbia 
16
and an appeal from the decision of the district court 
17
may be taken to the Court of Appeals for the Dis-
18
trict of Columbia Circuit. 
19
(2) The party filing the action shall concur-
20
rently deliver a copy the complaint to the Clerk of 
21
the House of Representatives and the Secretary of 
22
the Senate. 
23
(3) It shall be the duty of the United States 
24
District Court for the District of Columbia, the 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
23 
•HR 1 IH
Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Cir-
1
cuit, and the Supreme Court of the United States to 
2
advance on the docket and to expedite to the great-
3
est possible extent the disposition of the action and 
4
appeal. 
5
(b) INTERVENTION BY MEMBERS OF CONGRESS.—In 
6
any action described in subsection (a), any Member of the 
7
House of Representatives (including a Delegate or Resi-
8
dent Commissioner to the Congress) or Senate shall have 
9
the right to intervene either in support of or opposition 
10
to the position of a party to the case regarding the con-
11
stitutionality of the provision. To avoid duplication of ef-
12
forts and reduce the burdens placed on the parties to the 
13
action, the court in any such action may make such orders 
14
as it considers necessary, including orders to require 
15
interveners taking similar positions to file joint papers or 
16
to be represented by a single attorney at oral argument. 
17

SEC. 1000. SHORT TITLE; STATEMENT OF POLICY. 
1
(a) SHORT TITLE.—This title may be cited as the 
2
‘‘Voter Empowerment Act of 2021’’. 
3
(b) STATEMENT OF POLICY.—It is the policy of the 
4
United States that— 
5
(1) all eligible citizens of the United States 
6
should access and exercise their constitutional right 
7
to vote in a free, fair, and timely manner; and 
8
(2) the integrity, security, and accountability of 
9
the voting process must be vigilantly protected, 
10
maintained, and enhanced in order to protect and 
11
preserve electoral and participatory democracy in the 
12
United States.

PART 2—AUTOMATIC VOTER REGISTRATION 
3
SEC. 1011. SHORT TITLE; FINDINGS AND PURPOSE. 
4
(a) SHORT TITLE.—This part may be cited as the 
5
‘‘Automatic Voter Registration Act of 2021’’. 
6
(b) FINDINGS AND PURPOSE.— 
7
(1) FINDINGS.—Congress finds that— 
8
(A) the right to vote is a fundamental 
9
right of citizens of the United States; 
10
(B) it is the responsibility of the State and 
11
Federal Governments to ensure that every eligi-
12
ble citizen is registered to vote; 
13
(C) existing voter registration systems can 
14
be inaccurate, costly, inaccessible and con-
15
fusing, with damaging effects on voter partici-
16
pation in elections for Federal office and dis-
17
proportionate impacts on young people, persons 
18
with disabilities, and racial and ethnic minori-
19
ties; and 
20
(D) voter registration systems must be up-
21
dated with 21st Century technologies and pro-
22
cedures to maintain their security. 
23
(2) PURPOSE.—It is the purpose of this part— 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
47 
•HR 1 IH
(A) to establish that it is the responsibility 
1
of government at every level to ensure that all 
2
eligible citizens are registered to vote in elec-
3
tions for Federal office; 
4
(B) to enable the State and Federal Gov-
5
ernments to register all eligible citizens to vote 
6
with accurate, cost-efficient, and up-to-date pro-
7
cedures; 
8
(C) to modernize voter registration and list 
9
maintenance procedures with electronic and 
10
internet capabilities; and 
11
(D) to protect and enhance the integrity, 
12
accuracy, efficiency, and accessibility of the 
13
electoral process for all eligible citizens. 
14
SEC. 1012. AUTOMATIC REGISTRATION OF ELIGIBLE INDI-
15
VIDUALS. 
16
(a) REQUIRING STATES TO ESTABLISH AND OPER-
17
ATE AUTOMATIC REGISTRATION SYSTEM.— 
18
(1) IN GENERAL.—The chief State election offi-
19
cial of each State shall establish and operate a sys-
20
tem of automatic registration for the registration of 
21
eligible individuals to vote for elections for Federal 
22
office in the State, in accordance with the provisions 
23
of this part. 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
48 
•HR 1 IH
(2) DEFINITION.—The term ‘‘automatic reg-
1
istration’’ means a system that registers an indi-
2
vidual to vote in elections for Federal office in a 
3
State, if eligible, by electronically transferring the 
4
information necessary for registration from govern-
5
ment agencies to election officials of the State so 
6
that, unless the individual affirmatively declines to 
7
be registered, the individual will be registered to vote 
8
in such elections. 
9
(b) REGISTRATION
OF VOTERS BASED
ON NEW 
10
AGENCY RECORDS.—The chief State election official 
11

15

16

17
to be registered to vote in such elections; and 
18
(2) not later than 120 days after a contributing 
19
agency has transmitted such information with re-
20
spect to the individual, send written notice to the in-
21
dividual, in addition to other means of notice estab-
22
lished by this part, of the individual’s voter registra-
23
tion status. 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
49 
•HR 1 IH

•HR 1 IH

(d) TREATMENT OF INDIVIDUALS UNDER 18 YEARS

SEC. 1013. CONTRIBUTING AGENCY ASSISTANCE IN REG-
1
ISTRATION. 
2
(a) IN GENERAL.—In accordance with this part, each 
3
contributing agency in a State shall assist the State’s chief 
4

5
served by that agency. 
6
(b) REQUIREMENTS
FOR
CONTRIBUTING
AGEN-
7
CIES.— 
8
(1) INSTRUCTIONS ON AUTOMATIC REGISTRA-
9
TION.—With each application for service or assist-
10

11

12

13

15

16
States citizenship (either directly or as part of the 
17
overall application for service or assistance) shall in-
18
form each such individual who is a citizen of the 
19
United States of the following: 
20
(A) Unless that individual declines to reg-
21
ister to vote, or is found ineligible to vote, the 
22

23
cable, the individual’s registration will be up-
24
dated. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
53 
•HR 1 IH
(B) The substantive qualifications of an 
1

2

3
Federal office prescribed pursuant to section 9 
4
of the National Voter Registration Act of 1993, 
5
the consequences of false registration, and the 
6
individual should decline to register if the indi-
7
vidual does not meet all those qualifications. 
8
(C) In the case of a State in which affili-
9
ation or enrollment with a political party is re-
10
quired in order to participate in an election to 
11
select the party’s candidate in an election for 
12
Federal office, the requirement that the indi-
13

14
party in order to participate in such an election. 
15
(D) Voter registration is voluntary, and 
16
neither registering nor declining to register to 
17
vote will in any way affect the availability of 
18
services or benefits, nor be used for other pur-
19
poses. 
20
(2) OPPORTUNITY TO DECLINE REGISTRATION

REQUIRED.—Each contributing agency shall ensure

that each application for service or assistance, and

24

25

•HR 1 IH

1

be registered to vote. 
4
(3) INFORMATION
TRANSMITTAL.—Upon the 
5
expiration of the 30-day period which begins on the 
6
date the contributing agency informs the individual 
7
of the information described in paragraph (1), each 
8

SEC. 1015. VOTER PROTECTION AND SECURITY IN AUTO-
14
MATIC REGISTRATION. 
15
(a) PROTECTIONS FOR ERRORS IN REGISTRATION.— 
16
An individual shall not be prosecuted under any Federal 
17
or State law, adversely affected in any civil adjudication 
18
concerning immigration status or naturalization, or sub-
19
ject to an allegation in any legal proceeding that the indi-
20

23

18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
63 
•HR 1 IH

1

2

3
(3) The individual was automatically registered 
4
to vote under this part at an incorrect address. 
5
(4) The individual declined the opportunity to 
6
register to vote or did not make an affirmation of 
7
citizenship, including through automatic registration, 
8
under this part. 
9
(b) LIMITS
ON USE
OF AUTOMATIC REGISTRA-
10

12

16

18
alone.

(2) updates to online or electronic voter reg-

(3) introduction of online voter registration sys-
18

SEC. 1201. VOTER CAGING AND OTHER QUESTIONABLE 
17
CHALLENGES PROHIBITED. 
18
(a) IN GENERAL.—Chapter 29 of title 18, United 
19
States Code, as amended by section 1071(a), is amended 
20
by adding at the end the following: 
21
‘‘§ 613. Voter caging and other questionable chal-
22
lenges 
23
‘‘(a) DEFINITIONS.—In this section— 
24
‘‘(1) the term ‘voter caging document’ means— 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
117 
•HR 1 IH
‘‘(A) a nonforwardable document that is 
1
returned to the sender or a third party as unde-
2
livered or undeliverable despite an attempt to 
3
deliver such document to the address of a reg-
4
istered voter or applicant; or 
5
‘‘(B) any document with instructions to an 
6
addressee that the document be returned to the 
7
sender or a third party but is not so returned, 
8
despite an attempt to deliver such document to 
9
the address of a registered voter or applicant, 
10
unless at least two Federal election cycles have 
11
passed since the date of the attempted delivery; 
12
‘‘(2) the term ‘voter caging list’ means a list of 
13
individuals compiled from voter caging documents; 
14
and 
15
‘‘(3) the term ‘unverified match list’ means a 
16
list produced by matching the information of reg-
17
istered voters or applicants for voter registration to 
18
a list of individuals who are ineligible to vote in the 
19
registrar’s jurisdiction, by virtue of death, convic-
20
tion, change of address, or otherwise; unless one of 
21
the pieces of information matched includes a signa-
22
ture, photograph, or unique identifying number en-
23
suring that the information from each source refers 
24
to the same individual. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
118 
•HR 1 IH
‘‘(b) PROHIBITION AGAINST VOTER CAGING.—No 
1
State or local election official shall prevent an individual 
2
from registering or voting in any election for Federal of-
3
fice, or permit in connection with any election for Federal 
4
office a formal challenge under State law to an individual’s 
5
registration status or eligibility to vote, if the basis for 
6
such decision is evidence consisting of— 
7
‘‘(1) a voter caging document or voter caging 
8
list; 
9
‘‘(2) an unverified match list; 
10
‘‘(3) an error or omission on any record or 
11
paper relating to any application, registration, or 
12

13
sion is not material to an individual’s eligibility to 
14
vote under section 2004 of the Revised Statutes, as 
15
amended (52 U.S.C. 10101(a)(2)(B)); or 
16
‘‘(4) any other evidence so designated for pur-
17
poses of this section by the Election Assistance Com-
18
mission, 
19
except that the election official may use such evidence if 
20
it is corroborated by independent evidence of the individ-
21
ual’s ineligibility to register or vote. 
22

OTHER THAN ELECTION OFFICIALS.— 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1

•HR 1 IH

‘‘(d) PENALTIES

SEC. 1303. CORRECTIVE ACTION. 
5
(a) CORRECTIVE ACTION.— 
6
(1) IN GENERAL.—If the Attorney General re-
7

8
tion has been or is being communicated in violation 
9

10

11

12

13

14
rate information to correct the materially false infor-
15
mation, the Attorney General shall, pursuant to the 
16
written procedures and standards under subsection 
17
(b), communicate to the public, by any means, in-
18
cluding by means of written, electronic, or telephonic 
19
communications, accurate information designed to 
20
correct the materially false information. 
21
(2) COMMUNICATION OF CORRECTIVE INFORMA-
22
TION.—Any information communicated by the Attor-
23
ney General under paragraph (1)— 
24
(A) shall— 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
132 
•HR 1 IH
(i) be accurate and objective; 
1
(ii) consist of only the information 
2
necessary to correct the materially false in-
3
formation that has been or is being com-
4
municated; and 
5
(iii) to the extent practicable, be by a 
6
means that the Attorney General deter-
7
mines will reach the persons to whom the 
8
materially false information has been or is 
9
being communicated; and 
10
(B) shall not be designed to favor or dis-

12
political party.

Subtitle E—Democracy Restoration 
8
SEC. 1401. SHORT TITLE. 
9
This subtitle may be cited as the ‘‘Democracy Res-
10
toration Act of 2021’’. 
11
SEC. 1402. FINDINGS. 
12
Congress makes the following findings: 
13
(1) The right to vote is the most basic constitu-
14
tive act of citizenship. Regaining the right to vote 
15
reintegrates individuals with criminal convictions 
16
into free society, helping to enhance public safety. 
17
(2) Article I, section 4, of the Constitution 
18
grants Congress ultimate supervisory power over 
19
Federal elections, an authority which has repeatedly 
20
been upheld by the Supreme Court. 
21
(3) Basic constitutional principles of fairness 
22
and equal protection require an equal opportunity 
23
for citizens of the United States to vote in Federal 
24
elections. The right to vote may not be abridged or 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
137 
•HR 1 IH
denied by the United States or by any State on ac-
1
count of race, color, gender, or previous condition of 
2
servitude. The 13th, 14th, 15th, 19th, 24th, and 
3
26th Amendments to the Constitution empower Con-
4
gress to enact measures to protect the right to vote 
5
in Federal elections. The 8th Amendment to the 
6
Constitution provides for no excessive bail to be re-
7
quired, nor excessive fines imposed, nor cruel and 
8
unusual punishments inflicted. 
9
(4) There are 3 areas in which discrepancies in 
10
State laws regarding criminal convictions lead to un-
11
fairness in Federal elections: 
12
(A) The lack of a uniform standard for 
13
voting in Federal elections leads to an unfair 
14
disparity and unequal participation in Federal 
15
elections based solely on where a person lives. 
16
(B) Laws governing the restoration of vot-
17
ing rights after a criminal conviction vary 
18
throughout the country, and persons in some 
19
States can easily regain their voting rights 
20
while in other States persons effectively lose 
21
their right to vote permanently. 
22
(C) State disenfranchisement laws dis-
23
proportionately impact racial and ethnic minori-
24
ties. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
138 
•HR 1 IH
(5) Two States (Maine and Vermont), the Dis-
1
trict of Columbia, and the Commonwealth of Puerto 
2
Rico do not disenfranchise individuals with criminal 
3
convictions at all, but 48 States have laws that deny 
4
convicted individuals the right to vote while they are 
5
in prison. 
6
(6) In some States disenfranchisement results 
7
from varying State laws that restrict voting while in-
8
dividuals are under the supervision of the criminal 
9
justice system or after they have completed a crimi-
10
nal sentence. In 30 States, convicted individuals may 
11
not vote while they are on parole and 27 States dis-
12
enfranchise individuals on felony probation as well. 
13
In 11 States, a conviction can result in lifetime dis-
14
enfranchisement. 
15
(7) Several States deny the right to vote to in-
16
dividuals convicted of certain misdemeanors. 
17
(8) An estimated 5,200,000 citizens of the 
18
United States, or about 1 in 44 adults in the United 
19
States, currently cannot vote as a result of a felony 
20
conviction. Of the 5,200,000 citizens barred from 
21
voting, only 24 percent are in prison. By contrast, 
22
75 percent of the disenfranchised reside in their 
23
communities while on probation or parole or after 
24
having completed their sentences. Approximately 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
139 
•HR 1 IH
2,200,000 citizens who have completed their sen-
1
tences remain disenfranchised due to restrictive 
2
State laws. In at least 6 States—Alabama, Florida, 
3
Kentucky, Mississippi, Tennessee, and Virginia— 
4
more than 5 percent of the total voting-age popu-
5
lation is disenfranchised. 
6
(9) In those States that disenfranchise individ-
7
uals post-sentence, the right to vote can be regained 
8
in theory, but in practice this possibility is often 
9
granted in a non-uniform and potentially discrimina-
10
tory manner. Disenfranchised individuals must ei-
11
ther obtain a pardon or an order from the Governor 
12
or an action by the parole or pardon board, depend-
13
ing on the offense and State. Individuals convicted 
14
of a Federal offense often have additional barriers to 
15
regaining voting rights. 
16
(10) State disenfranchisement laws dispropor-
17
tionately impact racial and ethnic minorities. More 
18
than 6 percent of the African-American voting-age 
19
population, or 1,800,000 African Americans, are 
20
disenfranchised. Currently, 1 of every 16 voting-age 
21
African Americans are rendered unable to vote be-
22
cause of felony disenfranchisement, which is a rate 
23
more than 3.7 times greater than non-African Amer-
24
icans. Over 6 percent of African-American adults are 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
140 
•HR 1 IH
disenfranchised whereas only 1.7 percent of non-Af-
1
rican Americans are. In 7 States (Alabama, 16 per-
2
cent; Florida, 15 percent; Kentucky, 15 percent; 
3
Mississippi, 16 percent; Tennessee, 21 percent; Vir-
4
ginia, 16 percent; and Wyoming, 36 percent), more 
5
than 1 in 7 African Americans are unable to vote 
6
because of prior convictions, twice the national aver-
7
age for African Americans. 
8
(11) Latino citizens are disproportionately 
9
disenfranchised based upon their disproportionate 
10
representation in the criminal justice system. In re-
11
cent years, Latinos have been imprisoned at 2.5 
12
times the rate of Whites. More than 2 percent of the 
13
voting-age Latino population, or 560,000 Latinos, 
14
are disenfranchised due to a felony conviction. In 34 
15
states Latinos are disenfranchised at a higher rate 
16
than the general population. In 11 states 4 percent 
17
or more of Latino adults are disenfranchised due to 
18
a felony conviction (Alabama, 4 percent; Arizona, 7 
19
percent; Arkansas, 4 percent; Idaho, 4 percent; 
20
Iowa, 4 percent; Kentucky, 6 percent; Minnesota, 4 
21
percent; Mississippi, 5 percent; Nebraska, 6 percent; 
22
Tennessee, 11 percent, Wyoming, 4 percent), twice 
23
the national average for Latinos. 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
141 
•HR 1 IH
(12) Disenfranchising citizens who have been 
1
convicted of a criminal offense and who are living 
2
and working in the community serves no compelling 
3
State interest and hinders their rehabilitation and 
4
reintegration into society. 
5
(13) State disenfranchisement laws can sup-
6
press electoral participation among eligible voters by 
7
discouraging voting among family and community 
8
members of disenfranchised persons. Future elec-
9
toral participation by the children of disenfranchised 
10
parents may be impacted as well. 
11
(14) The United States is the only Western de-
12
mocracy that permits the permanent denial of voting 
13
rights for individuals with felony convictions. 
14
SEC. 1403. RIGHTS OF CITIZENS. 
15
The right of an individual who is a citizen of the 
16
United States to vote in any election for Federal office 
17
shall not be denied or abridged because that individual has 
18
been convicted of a criminal offense unless such individual 
19
is serving a felony sentence in a correctional institution 
20
or facility at the time of the election. 
21
SEC. 1404. ENFORCEMENT. 
22
(a) ATTORNEY GENERAL.—The Attorney General 
23
may, in a civil action, obtain such declaratory or injunctive 
24
relief as is necessary to remedy a violation of this subtitle. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
142 
•HR 1 IH
(b) PRIVATE RIGHT OF ACTION.— 
1
(1) IN GENERAL.—A person who is aggrieved 
2
by a violation of this subtitle may provide written 
3
notice of the violation to the chief election official of 
4
the State involved. 
5
(2) RELIEF.—Except as provided in paragraph 
6
(3), if the violation is not corrected within 90 days 
7
after receipt of a notice under paragraph (1), or 
8
within 20 days after receipt of the notice if the viola-
9
tion occurred within 120 days before the date of an 
10
election for Federal office, the aggrieved person 
11
may, in a civil action, obtain declaratory or injunc-
12
tive relief with respect to the violation. 
13
(3) EXCEPTION.—If the violation occurred 
14
within 30 days before the date of an election for 
15
Federal office, the aggrieved person need not provide 
16
notice to the chief election official of the State under 
17
paragraph (1) before bringing a civil action to obtain 
18
declaratory or injunctive relief with respect to the 
19
violation. 
20

SEC. 1406. DEFINITIONS. 
15
For purposes of this subtitle: 
16
(1) CORRECTIONAL
INSTITUTION
OR
FACIL-
17
ITY.—The term ‘‘correctional institution or facility’’ 
18
means any prison, penitentiary, jail, or other institu-
19
tion or facility for the confinement of individuals 
20
convicted of criminal offenses, whether publicly or 
21
privately operated, except that such term does not 
22
include any residential community treatment center 
23
(or similar public or private facility). 
24
(2) ELECTION.—The term ‘‘election’’ means— 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
146 
•HR 1 IH
(A) a general, special, primary, or runoff 
1
election; 
2
(B) a convention or caucus of a political 
3
party held to nominate a candidate; 
4
(C) a primary election held for the selec-
5
tion of delegates to a national nominating con-
6
vention of a political party; or 
7
(D) a primary election held for the expres-
8
sion of a preference for the nomination of per-
9
sons for election to the office of President. 
10
(3) FEDERAL OFFICE.—The term ‘‘Federal of-
11
fice’’ means the office of President or Vice President 
12
of the United States, or of Senator or Representa-
13
tive in, or Delegate or Resident Commissioner to, 
14
the Congress of the United States. 
15
(4) PROBATION.—The term ‘‘probation’’ means 
16
probation, imposed by a Federal, State, or local 
17
court, with or without a condition on the individual 
18
involved concerning— 
19
(A) the individual’s freedom of movement; 
20
(B) the payment of damages by the indi-
21
vidual; 
22
(C) periodic reporting by the individual to 
23
an officer of the court; or 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
147 
•HR 1 IH
(D) supervision of the individual by an of-
1
ficer of the court. 
2
SEC. 1407. RELATION TO OTHER LAWS. 
3
(a) STATE LAWS RELATING TO VOTING RIGHTS.— 
4
Nothing in this subtitle be construed to prohibit the States 
5
from enacting any State law which affords the right to 
6
vote in any election for Federal office on terms less restric-
7
tive than those established by this subtitle. 
8
(b) CERTAIN FEDERAL ACTS.—The rights and rem-
9
edies established by this subtitle are in addition to all 
10
other rights and remedies provided by law, and neither 
11
rights and remedies established by this Act shall super-
12
sede, restrict, or limit the application of the Voting Rights 
13
Act of 1965 (52 U.S.C. 10301 et seq.) or the National 
14
Voter Registration Act of 1993 (52 U.S.C. 20501 et seq.). 
15
SEC. 1408. FEDERAL PRISON FUNDS. 
16
No State, unit of local government, or other person 
17
may receive or use, to construct or otherwise improve a 
18
prison, jail, or other place of incarceration, any Federal 
19
funds unless that person has in effect a program under 
20
which each individual incarcerated in that person’s juris-
21
diction who is a citizen of the United States is notified, 
22
upon release from such incarceration, of that individual’s 
23
rights under section 1403. 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
148 
•HR 1 IH
SEC. 1409. EFFECTIVE DATE. 
1
This subtitle shall apply to citizens of the United 
2
States voting in any election for Federal office held after 
3
the date of the enactment of this Act.

10

11

16

ON
WHETHER
VOTE
WAS

21

22

24
not counted, the reasons therefor.

18:47 Jan 12, 2021

SEC. 1904. ACCOMMODATIONS FOR VOTERS RESIDING IN 
18
INDIAN LANDS.

(a) ACCOMMODATIONS DESCRIBED.— 
20
(1) DESIGNATION OF BALLOT PICKUP AND COL-
21

18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1

5

6

7

8
in Indian lands 60 days before the election.

OF
MAIL-IN
AND
ABSENTEE

11

13

15

18

20

21

25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1

•HR 1 IH

Subtitle A—Findings Reaffirming 
1
Commitment of Congress to Re-
2
store the Voting Rights Act 
3
SEC. 2001. FINDINGS REAFFIRMING COMMITMENT OF CON-
4
GRESS TO RESTORE THE VOTING RIGHTS 
5
ACT. 
6
Congress finds the following: 
7
(1) The right to vote for all Americans is sac-
8
rosanct and rules for voting and election administra-
9
tion should protect the right to vote and promote 
10
voter participation. 
11
(2) The Voting Rights Act has empowered the 
12
Department of Justice and Federal courts for nearly 
13
a half a century to block discriminatory voting prac-
14
tices before their implementation in States and local-
15
ities with the most troubling histories and ongoing 
16
records of racial discrimination. 
17
(3) There continues to be an alarming move-
18
ment to erect barriers to make it more difficult for 
19
Americans to participate in our Nation’s democratic 
20
process. The Nation has witnessed unprecedented ef-
21
forts to turn back the clock and erect barriers to 
22
voting for communities of color which have faced 
23
historic and continuing discrimination, as well as 
24
disabled, young, elderly, and low-income Americans. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
269 
•HR 1 IH
(4) The Supreme Court’s 2013 Shelby County 
1
v. Holder decision gutted decades-long Federal pro-
2
tections for communities of color that face historic 
3
and continuing discrimination, emboldening States 
4
and local jurisdictions to pass voter suppression laws 
5
and implement procedures, such as those requiring 
6
photo identification, limiting early voting hours, 
7
eliminating same-day registration, purging voters 
8
from the rolls, and reducing the number of polling 
9
places. Congress is committed to reversing the dev-
10
astating impact of this decision. 
11
(5) Racial discrimination in voting is a clear 
12
and persistent problem. The actions of States and 
13
localities around the country post-Shelby County, in-
14
cluding at least 10 findings by Federal courts of in-
15
tentional discrimination, underscore the need for 
16
Congress to conduct investigatory and evidentiary 
17
hearings to determine the legislation necessary to re-
18
store the Voting Rights Act and combat continuing 
19
efforts in America that suppress the free exercise of 
20
the franchise in communities of color. 
21
(6) The 2018 midterm and 2020 general elec-
22
tions provide further evidence that systemic voter 
23
discrimination and intimidation continues to occur in 
24
communities of color across the country, making it 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
270 
•HR 1 IH
clear that democracy reform cannot be achieved 
1
until Congress restores key provisions of the Voting 
2
Rights Act. 
3
(7) Congress must remain vigilant in protecting 
4
every eligible citizen’s right to vote. Congress should 
5
respond by modernizing the electoral system to— 
6
(A) improve access to the ballot; 
7
(B) enhance the integrity and security of 
8
our voting systems; 
9
(C) ensure greater accountability for the 
10
administration of elections; 
11
(D) restore protections for voters against 
12
practices in States and localities plagued by the 
13
persistence of voter disenfranchisement; and 
14
(E) ensure that Federal civil rights laws 
15
protect the rights of voters against discrimina-
16
tory and deceptive practices. 
17
Subtitle B—Findings Relating to 
18
Native American Voting Rights 
19
SEC. 2101. FINDINGS RELATING TO NATIVE AMERICAN VOT-
20
ING RIGHTS. 
21
Congress finds the following: 
22
(1) The right to vote for all Americans is sa-
23
cred. Congress must fulfill the Federal Government’s 
24
trust responsibility to protect and promote Native 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
271 
•HR 1 IH
Americans’ exercise of their fundamental right to 
1
vote, including equal access to voter registration vot-
2
ing mechanisms and locations, and the ability to 
3
serve as election officials. 
4
(2) The Native American Voting Rights Coali-
5
tion’s four-State survey of voter discrimination 
6
(2016) and nine field hearings in Indian Country 
7
(2017–2018) revealed obstacles that Native Ameri-
8
cans must overcome, including a lack of accessible 
9
and proximate registration and polling sites, non-
10
traditional addresses for residents on Indian reserva-
11
tions, inadequate language assistance for Tribal 
12
members, and voter identification laws that discrimi-
13
nate against Native Americans. The Department of 
14
Justice and courts have recognized that some juris-
15
dictions have been unresponsive to reasonable re-
16
quests from federally recognized Indian Tribes for 
17
more accessible and proximate voter registration 
18
sites and in-person voting locations. 
19
(3) The 2018 midterm and 2020 general elec-
20
tions provide further evidence that systemic voter 
21
discrimination and intimidation continues to occur in 
22
communities of color and Tribal lands across the 
23
country, making it clear that democracy reform can-
24
not be achieved until Congress restores key provi-
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
272 
•HR 1 IH
sions of the Voting Rights Act and passes additional 
1
protections. 
2
(4) Congress has broad, plenary authority to 
3
enact legislation to safeguard the voting rights of 
4
Native American voters. 
5
(5) Congress must conduct investigatory and 
6
evidentiary hearings to determine the necessary leg-
7
islation to restore the Voting Rights Act and combat 
8
continuous efforts that suppress the voter franchise 
9
within Tribal lands, to include, but not to be limited 
10
to, the Native American Voting Rights Act 
11
(NAVRA) and the Voting Rights Advancement Act 
12
(VRAA). 
13
Subtitle C—Findings Relating to 
14
District of Columbia Statehood 
15
SEC. 2201. FINDINGS RELATING TO DISTRICT OF COLUMBIA 
16
STATEHOOD. 
17
Congress finds the following: 
18
(1) The 705,000 District of Columbia residents 
19
deserve voting representation in Congress and local 
20
self-government, which only statehood can provide. 
21
(2) The United States is the only democratic 
22
country that denies both voting representation in the 
23
national legislature and local self-government to the 
24
residents of its Nation’s capital. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
273 
•HR 1 IH
(3) There are no constitutional, historical, fis-
1
cal, or economic reasons why the Americans who live 
2
in the District of Columbia should not be granted 
3
statehood. 
4
(4) Since the founding of the United States, the 
5
residents of the District of Columbia have always 
6
carried all of the obligations of citizenship, including 
7
serving in all of the Nation’s wars and paying Fed-
8
eral taxes, but have been denied voting representa-
9
tion in Congress and freedom from congressional in-
10
terference in purely local matters. 
11
(5) The District of Columbia pays more Federal 
12
taxes per capita than any State and more Federal 
13
taxes than 22 States. 
14
(6) The District of Columbia has a larger popu-
15
lation than 2 States (Wyoming and Vermont), and 
16
6 States have a population under one million. 
17
(7) The District of Columbia has a larger budg-
18
et than 12 States. 
19
(8) The Constitution of the United States gives 
20
Congress the authority to admit new States (clause 
21
1, section 3, article IV) and reduce the size of the 
22
seat of the Government of the United States (clause 
23
17, section 8, article I). All 37 new States have been 
24
admitted by an Act of Congress, and Congress has 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
274 
•HR 1 IH
previously reduced the size of the seat of the Gov-
1
ernment of the United States. 
2
(9) On June 26, 2020, by a vote of 232–180, 
3
the House of Representatives passed H.R. 51, the 
4
Washington, D.C. Admission Act, which would have 
5
admitted the State of Washington, Douglass Com-
6
monwealth from the residential portions of the Dis-
7
trict of Columbia and reduced the size of the seat 
8
of the Government of the United States to the 
9
United States Capitol, the White House, the United 
10
States Supreme Court, the National Mall, and the 
11
principal Federal monuments and buildings. 
12
Subtitle D—Territorial Voting 
13
Rights 
14
SEC. 2301. FINDINGS RELATING TO TERRITORIAL VOTING 
15
RIGHTS. 
16
Congress finds the following: 
17
(1) The right to vote is one of the most power-
18
ful instruments residents of the territories of the 
19

Subtitle E—Redistricting Reform 
1
SEC. 2400. SHORT TITLE; FINDING OF CONSTITUTIONAL AU-
2
THORITY. 
3
(a) SHORT TITLE.—This subtitle may be cited as the 
4
‘‘Redistricting Reform Act of 2021’’. 
5
(b) FINDING
OF CONSTITUTIONAL AUTHORITY.— 
6
Congress finds that it has the authority to establish the 
7
terms and conditions States must follow in carrying out 
8
congressional redistricting after an apportionment of 
9
Members of the House of Representatives because— 
10
(1) the authority granted to Congress under ar-
11
ticle I, section 4 of the Constitution of the United 
12
States gives Congress the power to enact laws gov-
13
erning the time, place, and manner of elections for 
14
Members of the House of Representatives; and 
15
(2) the authority granted to Congress under 
16
section 5 of the fourteenth amendment to the Con-
17
stitution gives Congress the power to enact laws to 
18
enforce section 2 of such amendment, which requires 
19
Representatives to be apportioned among the several 
20
States according to their number. 
21
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1

•HR 1 IH

SEC. 2401. REQUIRING CONGRESSIONAL REDISTRICTING

4

Act of 2021’’.

(c) SPECIAL RULE FOR EXISTING COMMISSIONS.—

(4) MULTI-PARTISAN COMPOSITION.—Member-
1

3
didates received the most votes in the most recent 
4
statewide election for Federal office held in the 
5

6

7

8

9

10

11

12

13
mizing the division of communities of interest and a 
14

15
(6) PUBLIC INPUT.—Public hearings are held 
16
and comments from the public are accepted before 
17
a final map is approved. 
18
(7) BROAD-BASED SUPPORT FOR APPROVAL OF 
19
FINAL PLAN.—The approval of the final redistricting 
20
plan requires a majority vote of the members of the 
21
commission, including the support of at least one

member of each of the following:

(A) Members who are affiliated with the 
24
political party whose candidate received the

SEC. 2413. CRITERIA FOR REDISTRICTING PLAN; PUBLIC 
11
NOTICE AND INPUT. 
12
(a) DEVELOPMENT OF REDISTRICTING PLAN.— 
13
(1) CRITERIA.—Under the redistricting plan of 
14
a State, there shall be established single-member 
15
congressional districts using the following criteria as 
16
set forth in the following order of priority: 
17
(A) Districts shall comply with the United 
18
States Constitution, including the requirement 
19
that they equalize total population. 
20
(B) Districts shall comply with the Voting 
21
Rights Act of 1965 (52 U.S.C. 10301 et seq.) 
22
and all applicable Federal laws. 
23
(C) Districts shall provide racial, ethnic, 
24
and language minorities with an equal oppor-
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
312 
•HR 1 IH
tunity to participate in the political process and 
1
to elect candidates of choice and shall not dilute 
2
or diminish their ability to elect candidates of 
3
choice whether alone or in coalition with others. 
4
(D) Districts shall respect communities of 
5
interest, neighborhoods, and political subdivi-
6
sions to the extent practicable and after compli-
7
ance with the requirements of subparagraphs 
8
(A) through (C). A community of interest is de-
9
fined as an area with recognized similarities of 
10
interests, including but not limited to ethnic, 
11
racial, economic, tribal, social, cultural, geo-
12
graphic or historic identities. The term commu-
13
nities of interest may, in certain circumstances, 
14
include political subdivisions such as counties, 
15
municipalities, tribal lands and reservations, or 
16
school districts, but shall not include common 
17
relationships with political parties or political 
18
candidates. 
19
(2) NO FAVORING OR DISFAVORING OF POLIT-
20
ICAL PARTIES.— 
21
(A) PROHIBITION.—The redistricting plan 
22
developed by the independent redistricting com-
23
mission shall not, when considered on a state-
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
313 
•HR 1 IH
wide basis, unduly favor or disfavor any polit-
1
ical party. 
2
(B) DETERMINATION
OF
FAVORING
OR 
3
DISFAVORING.—For purposes of subparagraph 
4
(A), the determination of whether a redis-
5
tricting plan has the effect of unduly favoring 
6
or disfavoring a political party shall be based on 
7
the totality of circumstances, including, but not 
8
limited to, whether the plan results in durable 
9
partisan bias as determined by scientifically ac-
10
cepted measures of partisan fairness, or wheth-
11
er there are alternative plans that would have 
12
complied with the requirements of law and re-
13
sulted in less durable levels of partisan bias. 
14
Notwithstanding the previous sentence, no re-
15
districting plan shall be found to be in violation 
16
of subparagraph (A) because of application of 
17
the criteria set forth in subparagraphs (A), (B), 
18
or (C) of paragraph (1). 
19
(3) FACTORS
PROHIBITED
FROM
CONSIDER-
20
ATION.—In developing the redistricting plan for the 
21
State, the independent redistricting commission may 
22
not take into consideration any of the following fac-
23
tors, except to the extent necessary to comply with 
24
the criteria described in subparagraphs (A) through 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
314 
•HR 1 IH
(C) of paragraph (1), paragraph (2), and to enable 
1
the redistricting plan to be measured against the ex-
2
ternal metrics described in subsection (e): 
3
(A) The residence of any Member of the 
4
House of Representatives or candidate. 
5
(B) The political party affiliation or voting 
6
history of the population of a district. 
7

ESS.—The independent redistricting commission of a

SEC. 2431. PAYMENTS TO STATES FOR CARRYING OUT RE-
8

9

12

(2) $150,000. 
18

19

20

21
ment the State redistricting plan, and to otherwise carry 
22
out congressional redistricting in the State. 
23

24

25

•HR 1 IH

(1) REQUIREMENT.—Except as provided in

12

on Redistricting for the State established under sec-
14
tion 2414(b). 
15
(2) EXCEPTION
FOR
STATES
WITH
EXISTING 
16

17
ant to section 2401(c), is exempt from the require-

19

20

21
tricting commission meets the requirements of sec-

(1) The action shall be filed in the district court 
6
of the United States for the District of Columbia or 
7
for the judicial district in which the capital of the 
8
State is located, as selected by the person bringing 
9
the action. 
10
(2) The action shall be heard by a 3-judge 
11
court convened pursuant to section 2284 of title 28, 
12
United States Code. 
13
(3) The 3-judge court shall consolidate actions 
14
brought for relief under subsection (b)(1) with re-
15
spect to the same State redistricting plan. 
16
(4) A copy of the complaint shall be delivered 
17
promptly to the Clerk of the House of Representa-
18
tives and the Secretary of the Senate. 
19
(5) A final decision in the action shall be re-
20
viewable only by appeal directly to the Supreme 
21
Court of the United States. Such appeal shall be 
22
taken by the filing of a notice of appeal within 10 
23
days, and the filing of a jurisdictional statement 
24
within 30 days, of the entry of the final decision.

‘‘SEC. 299. GRANTS FOR CONDUCTING RISK-LIMITING AU-
12
DITS OF RESULTS OF ELECTIONS. 
13
‘‘(a) AVAILABILITY OF GRANTS.—The Commission 
14
shall make a grant to each eligible State to conduct risk- 
15
limiting audits as described in subsection (b) with respect 
16
to the regularly scheduled general elections for Federal of-
17
fice held in November 2022 and each succeeding election 
18
for Federal office. 
19
‘‘(b) RISK-LIMITING AUDITS DESCRIBED.—In this 
20
part, a ‘risk-limiting audit’ is a post-election process— 
21
‘‘(1) which is conducted in accordance with 
22
rules and procedures established by the chief State 
23
election official of the State which meet the require-
24
ments of subsection (c); and 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
367 
•HR 1 IH
‘‘(2) under which, if the reported outcome of 
1
the election is incorrect, there is at least a predeter-
2
mined percentage chance that the audit will replace 
3
the incorrect outcome with the correct outcome as 
4
determined by a full, hand-to-eye tabulation of all 
5
votes validly cast in that election that ascertains 
6
voter intent manually and directly from voter- 
7
verifiable paper records. 
8
‘‘(c) REQUIREMENTS
FOR
RULES
AND
PROCE-
9
DURES.—The rules and procedures established for con-
10
ducting a risk-limiting audit shall include the following 
11
elements: 
12
‘‘(1) Rules for ensuring the security of ballots 
13
and documenting that prescribed procedures were 
14
followed. 
15
‘‘(2) Rules and procedures for ensuring the ac-
16
curacy of ballot manifests produced by election agen-
17
cies. 
18
‘‘(3) Rules and procedures for governing the 
19
format of ballot manifests, cast vote records, and 
20
other data involved in the audit. 
21
‘‘(4) Methods to ensure that any cast vote 
22
records used in the audit are those used by the vot-
23
ing system to tally the election results sent to the 
24
chief State election official and made public. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
368 
•HR 1 IH
‘‘(5) Procedures for the random selection of 
1
ballots to be inspected manually during each audit. 
2
‘‘(6) Rules for the calculations and other meth-
3
ods to be used in the audit and to determine wheth-
4
er and when the audit of an election is complete. 
5
‘‘(7) Procedures and requirements for testing 
6
any software used to conduct risk-limiting audits. 
7
‘‘(d) DEFINITIONS.—In this part, the following defi-
8
nitions apply: 
9
‘‘(1) The term ‘ballot manifest’ means a record 
10
maintained by each election agency that meets each 
11
of the following requirements: 
12
‘‘(A) The record is created without reliance 
13
on any part of the voting system used to tab-
14
ulate votes. 
15
‘‘(B) The record functions as a sampling 
16
frame for conducting a risk-limiting audit. 
17
‘‘(C) The record contains the following in-
18
formation with respect to the ballots cast and 
19
counted in the election: 
20
‘‘(i) The total number of ballots cast 
21
and counted by the agency (including 
22
undervotes, overvotes, and other invalid 
23
votes). 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
369 
•HR 1 IH
‘‘(ii) The total number of ballots cast 
1
in each election administered by the agency 
2
(including undervotes, overvotes, and other 
3
invalid votes).

‘‘(iii) A precise description of the 
5

6

7

8

9

‘‘(2) The term ‘incorrect outcome’ means an 
12

13

14

15

‘‘(3) The term ‘outcome’ means the winner of

SEC. 3201. NATIONAL STRATEGY TO PROTECT UNITED 
4
STATES DEMOCRATIC INSTITUTIONS. 
5
(a) IN GENERAL.—Not later than one year after the 
6
date of the enactment of this Act, the President, acting 
7
through the Secretary, in consultation with the Chairman, 
8
the Secretary of Defense, the Secretary of State, the At-
9
torney General, the Secretary of Education, the Director 
10
of National Intelligence, the Chairman of the Federal 
11

12

13

14
disinformation campaigns, and other activities that could 
15
undermine the security and integrity of United States 
16
democratic institutions. 
17
(b) CONSIDERATIONS.—The national strategy re-
18
quired under subsection (a) shall include consideration of 
19
the following: 
20
(1) The threat of a foreign state actor, foreign 
21
terrorist organization (as designated pursuant to 
22
section 219 of the Immigration and Nationality Act 
23
(8 U.S.C. 1189)), or a domestic actor carrying out 
24
a cyber attack, influence operation, disinformation 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
383 
•HR 1 IH
campaign, or other activity aimed at undermining 
1
the security and integrity of United States demo-
2
cratic institutions. 
3
(2) The extent to which United States demo-
4
cratic institutions are vulnerable to a cyber attack, 
5
influence operation, disinformation campaign, or 
6
other activity aimed at undermining the security and 
7
integrity of such democratic institutions. 
8
(3) Potential consequences, such as an erosion 
9
of public trust or an undermining of the rule of law, 
10
that could result from a successful cyber attack, in-
11
fluence operation, disinformation campaign, or other 
12
activity aimed at undermining the security and in-
13
tegrity of United States democratic institutions. 
14
(4) Lessons learned from other governments the 
15
institutions of which were subject to a cyber attack, 
16
influence operation, disinformation campaign, or 
17
other activity aimed at undermining the security and 
18
integrity of such institutions, as well as actions that 
19
could be taken by the United States Government to 
20
bolster collaboration with foreign partners to detect, 
21
deter, prevent, and counter such activities. 
22
(5) Potential impacts, such as an erosion of 
23
public trust in democratic institutions, as could be 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
384 
•HR 1 IH
associated with a successful cyber breach or other 
1

SEC. 4001. FINDINGS RELATING TO ILLICIT MONEY UNDER-
4
MINING OUR DEMOCRACY. 
5
Congress finds the following: 
6
(1) Criminals, terrorists, and corrupt govern-
7
ment officials frequently abuse anonymously held 
8
Limited Liability Companies (LLCs), also known as 
9
‘‘shell companies,’’ to hide, move, and launder the 
10
dirty money derived from illicit activities such as 
11
trafficking, bribery, exploitation, and embezzlement. 
12
Ownership and control of the finances that run 
13
through shell companies are obscured to regulators 
14
and law enforcement because little information is re-
15
quired and collected when establishing these entities. 
16
(2) The public release of the ‘‘Panama Papers’’ 
17
in 2016 and the ‘‘Paradise Papers’’ in 2017 revealed 
18
that these shell companies often purchase and sell 
19
United States real estate. United States anti-money 
20
laundering laws do not apply to cash transactions in-
21
volving real estate effectively concealing the bene-
22
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
411 
•HR 1 IH
ficiaries and transactions from regulators and law 
1
enforcement. 
2
(3) Since the Supreme Court’s decisions in Citi-
3
zens United v. Federal Election Commission, 558 
4
U.S. 310 (2010), millions of dollars have flowed into 
5
super PACs through LLCs whose funders are anon-
6
ymous or intentionally obscured. Criminal investiga-
7
tions have uncovered LLCs that were used to hide 
8
illegal campaign contributions from foreign criminal 
9
fugitives, to advance international influence-buying 
10
schemes, and to conceal contributions from donors 
11
who were already under investigation for bribery and 
12
racketeering. Voters have no way to know the true 
13
sources of the money being routed through these 
14
LLCs to influence elections, including whether any 
15
of the funds come from foreign or other illicit 
16
sources. 
17
(4) Congress should curb the use of anonymous 
18
shell companies for illicit purposes by requiring 
19
United States companies to disclose their beneficial 
20
owners, strengthening anti-money laundering and 
21
counter-terrorism finance laws. 
22
(5) Congress should examine the money laun-
23
dering and terrorist financing risks in the real estate 
24
market, including the role of anonymous parties, and 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
412 
•HR 1 IH
review legislation to address any vulnerabilities iden-
1
tified in this sector. 
2
(6) Congress should examine the methods by 
3
which corruption flourishes and the means to detect 
4
and deter the financial misconduct that fuels this 
5
driver of global instability. Congress should monitor 
6
government efforts to enforce United States anti- 
7
corruption laws and regulations. 
8

Subtitle 
C—Strengthening 
Over-
1
sight of Online Political Adver-
2
tising 
3
SEC. 4201. SHORT TITLE. 
4
This subtitle may be cited as the ‘‘Honest Ads Act’’. 
5
SEC. 4202. PURPOSE. 
6
The purpose of this subtitle is to enhance the integ-
7
rity of American democracy and national security by im-
8
proving disclosure requirements for online political adver-
9
tisements in order to uphold the Supreme Court’s well- 
10
established standard that the electorate bears the right to 
11
be fully informed. 
12
SEC. 4203. FINDINGS. 
13
Congress makes the following findings: 
14
(1) On January 6, 2017, the Office of the Di-
15
rector of National Intelligence published a report ti-
16
tled ‘‘Assessing Russian Activities and Intentions in 
17
Recent U.S. Elections’’, noting that ‘‘Russian Presi-
18
dent Vladimir Putin ordered an influence campaign 
19
in 2016 aimed at the US presidential election * * 
20
*’’. Moscow’s influence campaign followed a Russian 
21
messaging strategy that blends covert intelligence 
22
operation—such as cyber activity—with overt efforts 
23
by Russian Government agencies, state-funded 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
461 
•HR 1 IH
media, third-party intermediaries, and paid social 
1
media users or ‘‘trolls’’. 
2
(2) On November 24, 2016, The Washington 
3
Post reported findings from 2 teams of independent 
4
researchers that concluded Russians ‘‘exploited 
5
American-made technology platforms to attack U.S. 
6
democracy at a particularly vulnerable moment * * 
7
* as part of a broadly effective strategy of sowing 
8
distrust in U.S. democracy and its leaders.’’. 
9
(3) Findings from a 2017 study on the manipu-
10
lation of public opinion through social media con-
11
ducted by the Computational Propaganda Research 
12
Project at the Oxford Internet Institute found that 
13
the Kremlin is using pro-Russian bots to manipulate 
14
public discourse to a highly targeted audience. With 
15
a sample of nearly 1,300,000 tweets, researchers 
16
found that in the 2016 election’s 3 decisive States, 
17
propaganda constituted 40 percent of the sampled 
18
election-related tweets that went to Pennsylvanians, 
19
34 percent to Michigan voters, and 30 percent to 
20
those in Wisconsin. In other swing States, the figure 
21
reached 42 percent in Missouri, 41 percent in Flor-
22
ida, 40 percent in North Carolina, 38 percent in 
23
Colorado, and 35 percent in Ohio. 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
462 
•HR 1 IH
(4) On September 6, 2017, the Nation’s largest 
1
social media platform disclosed that between June 
2
2015 and May 2017, Russian entities purchased 
3
$100,000 in political advertisements, publishing 
4
roughly 3,000 ads linked to fake accounts associated 
5
with the Internet Research Agency, a pro-Kremlin 
6
organization. According to the company, the ads 
7
purchased focused ‘‘on amplifying divisive social and 
8
political messages * * *’’. 
9
(5) In 2002, the Bipartisan Campaign Reform 
10
Act became law, establishing disclosure requirements 
11
for political advertisements distributed from a tele-
12
vision or radio broadcast station or provider of cable 
13
or satellite television. In 2003, the Supreme Court 
14
upheld regulations on electioneering communications 
15
established under the Act, noting that such require-
16
ments ‘‘provide the electorate with information and 
17
insure that the voters are fully informed about the 
18
person or group who is speaking.’’. 
19
(6) According to a study from Borrell Associ-
20
ates, in 2016, $1,415,000,000 was spent on online 
21
advertising, more than quadruple the amount in 
22
2012. 
23
(7) The reach of a few large internet plat-
24
forms—larger than any broadcast, satellite, or cable 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
463 
•HR 1 IH
provider—has greatly facilitated the scope and effec-
1
tiveness of disinformation campaigns. For instance, 
2
the largest platform has over 210,000,000 Ameri-
3
cans users—over 160,000,000 of them on a daily 
4
basis. By contrast, the largest cable television pro-
5
vider has 22,430,000 subscribers, while the largest 
6
satellite television provider has 21,000,000 sub-
7
scribers. And the most-watched television broadcast 
8
in United States history had 118,000,000 viewers. 
9
(8) The public nature of broadcast television, 
10
radio, and satellite ensures a level of publicity for 
11
any political advertisement. These communications 
12
are accessible to the press, fact-checkers, and polit-
13
ical opponents; this creates strong disincentives for 
14
a candidate to disseminate materially false, inflam-
15
matory, or contradictory messages to the public. So-
16
cial media platforms, in contrast, can target portions 
17
of the electorate with direct, ephemeral advertise-
18
ments often on the basis of private information the 
19
platform has on individuals, enabling political adver-
20
tisements that are contradictory, racially or socially 
21
inflammatory, or materially false. 
22
(9) According to comScore, 2 companies own 8 
23
of the 10 most popular smart phone applications as 
24
of June 2017, including the most popular social 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
464 
•HR 1 IH
media and email services—which deliver information 
1
and news to users without requiring proactivity by 
2
the user. Those same 2 companies accounted for 99 
3
percent of revenue growth from digital advertising in 
4
2016, including 77 percent of gross spending. 79 
5
percent of online Americans—representing 68 per-
6
cent of all Americans—use the single largest social 
7
network, while 66 percent of these users are most 
8
likely to get their news from that site. 
9
(10) In its 2006 rulemaking, the Federal Elec-
10
tion Commission noted that only 18 percent of all 
11
Americans cited the internet as their leading source 
12
of news about the 2004 Presidential election; by con-
13
trast, the Pew Research Center found that 65 per-
14
cent of Americans identified an internet-based 
15
source as their leading source of information for the 
16
2016 election. 
17
(11) The Federal Election Commission, the 
18
independent Federal agency charged with protecting 
19
the integrity of the Federal campaign finance proc-
20
ess by providing transparency and administering 
21
campaign finance laws, has failed to take action to 
22
address online political advertisements. 
23
(12) In testimony before the Senate Select 
24
Committee on Intelligence titled, ‘‘Disinformation: A 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
465 
•HR 1 IH
Primer in Russian Active Measures and Influence 
1
Campaigns’’, multiple expert witnesses testified that 
2
while the disinformation tactics of foreign adver-
3
saries have not necessarily changed, social media 
4
services now provide ‘‘platform[s] practically pur-
5
pose-built for active measures[.]’’ Similarly, as Gen. 
6
Keith B. Alexander (RET.), the former Director of 
7
the National Security Agency, testified, during the 
8
Cold War ‘‘if the Soviet Union sought to manipulate 
9
information flow, it would have to do so principally 
10
through its own propaganda outlets or through ac-
11
tive measures that would generate specific news: 
12
planting of leaflets, inciting of violence, creation of 
13
other false materials and narratives. But the news 
14
itself was hard to manipulate because it would have 
15
required actual control of the organs of media, which 
16
took long-term efforts to penetrate. Today, however, 
17
because the clear majority of the information on so-
18
cial media sites is uncurated and there is a rapid 
19
proliferation of information sources and other sites 
20
that can reinforce information, there is an increasing 
21
likelihood that the information available to average 
22
consumers may be inaccurate (whether intentionally 
23
or otherwise) and may be more easily manipulable 
24
than in prior eras.’’. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
466 
•HR 1 IH
(13) Current regulations on political advertise-
1
ments do not provide sufficient transparency to up-
2
hold the public’s right to be fully informed about po-
3
litical advertisements made online. 
4
SEC. 4204. SENSE OF CONGRESS. 
5
It is the sense of Congress that— 
6
(1) the dramatic increase in digital political ad-
7
vertisements, and the growing centrality of online 
8
platforms in the lives of Americans, requires the 
9
Congress and the Federal Election Commission to 
10
take meaningful action to ensure that laws and reg-
11
ulations provide the accountability and transparency 
12
that is fundamental to our democracy; 
13
(2) free and fair elections require both trans-
14
parency and accountability which give the public a 
15
right to know the true sources of funding for polit-
16
ical advertisements in order to make informed polit-
17
ical choices and hold elected officials accountable; 
18
and 
19
(3) transparency of funding for political adver-
20
tisements is essential to enforce other campaign fi-
21
nance laws, including the prohibition on campaign 
22
spending by foreign nationals. 
23
18:47 Jan 12, 2021

•HR 1 IH

18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1

•HR 1 IH
(b) ELEMENTS.—The study and report under sub-
1

2

3

4

5

6

7

8

(e) DEFINITION OF MEDIA LITERACY.—The term 
3
‘‘media literacy’’ means the ability to— 
4
(1) access relevant and accurate information 
5
through media; 
6
(2) critically analyze media content and the in-
7
fluences of media; 
8
(3) evaluate the comprehensiveness, relevance, 
9

10
(4) make educated decisions based on informa-
11
tion obtained from media and digital sources; 
12
(5) operate various forms of technology and 
13
digital tools; and 
14
(6) reflect on how the use of media and tech-
15
nology may affect private and public life.

‘‘(a) IN GENERAL.—Except as provided in sub-

7
lllll has been manipulated.’’; and

‘‘(3) AUDIO-ONLY MEDIA.—If the media con-
22

•HR 1 IH

Subtitle A—Findings Relating to 
1
Citizens United Decision 
2
SEC. 5001. FINDINGS RELATING TO CITIZENS UNITED DECI-
3
SION. 
4
Congress finds the following: 
5
(1) The American Republic was founded on the 
6
principle that all people are created equal, with 
7
rights and responsibilities as citizens to vote, be rep-
8
resented, speak, debate, and participate in self-gov-
9
ernment on equal terms regardless of wealth. To se-
10
cure these rights and responsibilities, our Constitu-
11
tion not only protects the equal rights of all Ameri-
12
cans but also provides checks and balances to pre-
13
vent corruption and prevent concentrated power and 
14
wealth from undermining effective self-government. 
15
(2) The Founders designed the First Amend-
16
ment to help prevent tyranny by ensuring that the 
17
people have the tools they need to ensure self-gov-
18
ernment and to keep their elected leaders responsive 
19
to the public. The Amendment thus guarantees the 
20
right of everyone to speak, to petition the govern-
21
ment for redress, to assemble together, and for a 
22
free press. If only the wealthiest individuals can par-
23
ticipate meaningfully in our democracy, then these 
24
First Amendment principles become an illusion. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
533 
•HR 1 IH
(3) Campaign finance laws promote these First 
1
Amendment interests. They increase robust debate 
2
from diverse voices, enhance the responsiveness of 
3
elected officeholders, and help prevent corruption. 
4
They do not censor anyone’s speech but simply en-
5
sure that no one’s speech is drowned out. The Su-
6
preme Court has failed to recognize that these laws 
7
are essential, proactive rules that help guarantee 
8
true democratic self-government. 
9
(4) The Supreme Court’s decisions in Citizens 
10
United v. Federal Election Commission, 558 U.S. 
11
310 (2010) and McCutcheon v. FEC, 572 U.S. 185 
12
(2014), as well as other court decisions, erroneously 
13
invalidated even-handed rules about the spending of 
14
money in local, State, and Federal elections. These 
15
rules do not prevent anyone from speaking their 
16
mind, much less pick winners and losers of political 
17
debates. Although the Court has upheld other con-
18
tent-neutral laws like these, it has failed to apply to 
19
same logic to campaign finance laws. These flawed 
20
decisions have empowered large corporations, ex-
21
tremely wealthy individuals, and special interests to 
22
dominate election spending, corrupt our politics, and 
23
degrade our democracy through tidal waves of un-
24
limited and anonymous spending. These decisions 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
534 
•HR 1 IH
also stand in contrast to a long history of efforts by 
1
Congress and the States to regulate money in poli-
2
tics to protect democracy, and they illustrate a trou-
3
bling deregulatory trend in campaign finance-related 
4
court decisions. Additionally, an unknown amount of 
5
foreign money continues to be spent in our political 
6
system as subsidiaries of foreign-based corporations 
7
and hostile foreign actors sometimes connected to 
8
nation-states work to influence our elections. 
9
(5) The Supreme Court’s misinterpretation of 
10
the Constitution to empower monied interests at the 
11
expense of the American people in elections has seri-
12
ously eroded over 100 years of congressional action 
13
to promote fairness and protect elections from the 
14
toxic influence of money. 
15
(6) In 1907, Congress passed the Tillman Act 
16
in response to the concentration of corporate power 
17
in the post-Civil War Gilded Age. The Act prohibited 
18
corporations from making contributions in connec-
19
tion with Federal elections, aiming ‘‘not merely to 
20
prevent the subversion of the integrity of the elec-
21
toral process [but] * * * to sustain the active, alert 
22
responsibility of the individual citizen in a democ-
23
racy for the wise conduct of government’’. 
24
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
535 
•HR 1 IH
(7) By 1910, Congress began passing disclosure 
1
requirements and campaign expenditure limits, and 
2
dozens of States passed corrupt practices Acts to 
3
prohibit corporate spending in elections. States also 
4
enacted campaign spending limits, and some States 
5
limited the amount that people could contribute to 
6
campaigns. 
7
(8) In 1947, the Taft-Hartley Act prohibited 
8
corporations and unions from making campaign con-
9
tributions or other expenditures to influence elec-
10
tions. In 1962, a Presidential commission on election 
11
spending recommended spending limits and incen-
12
tives to increase small contributions from more peo-
13
ple. 
14
(9) The Federal Election Campaign Act of 
15
1971 (FECA), as amended in 1974, required disclo-
16
sure of contributions and expenditures, imposed con-
17
tribution and expenditure limits for individuals and 
18
groups, set spending limits for campaigns, can-
19
didates, and groups, implemented a public funding 
20
system for Presidential campaigns, and created the 
21
Federal Election Commission to oversee and enforce 
22
the new rules. 
23
(10) In the wake of Citizens United and other 
24
damaging Federal court decisions, Americans have 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
536 
•HR 1 IH
witnessed an explosion of outside spending in elec-
1
tions. Outside spending increased more than 700 
2
percent between the 2008 and 2020 Presidential 
3
election years. Spending by outside groups nearly 
4
doubled again from 2016 to 2020 with super PACs, 
5
tax-exempt groups, and others spending more than 
6
$3,000,000,000. And as political entities adapt to a 
7
post-Citizens United, post-McCutcheon landscape, 
8
these trends are getting worse, as evidenced by the 
9
record-setting 2020 elections which cost more than 
10
$14,000,000,000 in total. 
11
(11) Since the landmark Citizens United deci-
12
sion, 21 States and more than 800 municipalities, 
13
including large cities like New York, Los Angeles, 
14
Chicago, and Philadelphia, have gone on record sup-
15
porting a constitutional amendment. Transcending 
16
political leanings and geographic location, voters in 
17
States and municipalities across the country that 
18
have placed amendment questions on the ballot have 
19
routinely supported these initiatives by considerably 
20
large margins. 
21
(12) The Court has tied the hands of Congress 
22
and the States, severely restricting them from set-
23
ting reasonable limits on campaign spending. For 
24
example, the Court has held that only the Govern-
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
537 
•HR 1 IH
ment’s interest in preventing quid pro quo corrup-
1
tion, like bribery, or the appearance of such corrup-
2
tion, can justify limits on campaign contributions. 
3
More broadly, the Court has severely curtailed at-
4
tempts to reduce the ability of the Nation’s wealthi-
5
est and most powerful to skew our democracy in 
6
their favor by buying outsized influence in our elec-
7
tions. Because this distortion of the Constitution has 
8
prevented other critical regulation or reform of the 
9
way we finance elections in America, a constitutional 
10
amendment is needed to achieve a democracy for all 
11
the people. 
12
(13) The torrent of money flowing into our po-
13
litical system has a profound effect on the demo-
14
cratic process for everyday Americans, whose voices 
15
and policy preferences are increasingly being 
16
drowned out by those of wealthy special interests. 
17
The more campaign cash from wealthy special inter-
18
ests can flood our elections, the more policies that 
19
favor those interests are reflected in the national po-
20
litical agenda. When it comes to policy preferences, 
21
our Nation’s wealthiest tend to have fundamentally 
22
different views than do average Americans when it 
23
comes to issues ranging from unemployment benefits 
24
to the minimum wage to health care coverage. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
538 
•HR 1 IH
(14) At the same time millions of Americans 
1
have signed petitions, marched, called their Members 
2
of Congress, written letters to the editor, and other-
3
wise demonstrated their public support for a con-
4
stitutional amendment to overturn Citizens United 
5
that will allow Congress to reign in the outsized in-
6
fluence of unchecked money in politics. Dozens of 
7
organizations, representing tens of millions of indi-
8
viduals, have come together in a shared strategy of 
9
supporting such an amendment. 
10
(15) In order to protect the integrity of democ-
11
racy and the electoral process and to ensure political 
12
equality for all, the Constitution should be amended 
13
so that Congress and the States may regulate and 
14
set limits on the raising and spending of money to 
15
influence elections and may distinguish between nat-
16
ural persons and artificial entities, like corporations, 
17
that are created by law, including by prohibiting 
18
such artificial entities from spending money to influ-
19
ence elections. 
20
Subtitle B—Congressional 
21

PART 1—MY VOICE VOUCHER PILOT PROGRAM 
1
SEC. 5101. ESTABLISHMENT OF PILOT PROGRAM. 
2
(a) ESTABLISHMENT.—The Federal Election Com-
3
mission (hereafter in this part referred to as the ‘‘Commis-
4
sion’’) shall establish a pilot program under which the 
5
Commission shall select 3 eligible States to operate a 
6
voucher pilot program which is described in section 5102 
7
during the program operation period. 
8
(b) ELIGIBILITY OF STATES.—A State is eligible to 
9
be selected to operate a voucher pilot program under this 
10
part if, not later than 180 days after the beginning of the 
11
program application period, the State submits to the Com-
12
mission an application containing— 
13
(1) information and assurances that the State 
14
will operate a voucher program which contains the 
15
elements described in section 5102(a); 
16
(2) information and assurances that the State 
17
will establish fraud prevention mechanisms described 
18
in section 5102(b); 
19
(3) information and assurances that the State 
20
will establish a commission to oversee and implement 
21
the program as described in section 5102(c); 
22
(4) information and assurances that the State 
23
will carry out a public information campaign as de-
24
scribed in section 5102(d); 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1

•HR 1 IH
(5) information and assurances that the State 
1

2
and 
3
(6) such other information and assurances as 
4
the Commission may require. 
5
(c) SELECTION OF PARTICIPATING STATES.— 
6
(1) IN GENERAL.—Not later than 1 year after 
7
the beginning of the program application period, the 
8
Commission shall select the 3 States which will oper-
9
ate voucher pilot programs under this part. 
10
(2) CRITERIA.—In selecting States for the oper-
11
ation of the voucher pilot programs under this part, 
12
the Commission shall apply such criteria and metrics 
13
as the Commission considers appropriate to deter-
14
mine the ability of a State to operate the program 
15

16
variety of geographic regions and with a variety of 
17
political party preferences. 
18
(3) NO SUPERMAJORITY REQUIRED FOR SELEC-
19

20
under this subsection shall require the approval of 
21
only half of the Members of the Commission.

(d) DUTIES OF STATES DURING PROGRAM PREPARA-

each State selected to operate a voucher pilot program
