SEC. 5102. VOUCHER PROGRAM DESCRIBED. 
1
(a) GENERAL ELEMENTS OF PROGRAM.— 
2
(1) ELEMENTS DESCRIBED.—The elements of a 
3
voucher pilot program operated by a State under 
4
this part are as follows: 
5
(A) The State shall provide each qualified 
6
individual upon the individual’s request with a 
7
voucher worth $25 to be known as a ‘‘My Voice 
8
Voucher’’ during the election cycle which will be 
9
assigned a routing number and which at the op-
10
tion of the individual will be provided in either 
11
paper or electronic form. 
12
(B) Using the routing number assigned to 
13
the My Voice Voucher, the individual may sub-
14
mit the My Voice Voucher in either electronic 
15
or paper form to qualified candidates for elec-
16
tion for the office of Representative in, or Dele-
17
gate or Resident Commissioner to, the Congress 
18
and allocate such portion of the value of the My 
19
Voice Voucher in increments of $5 as the indi-
20
vidual may select to any such candidate. 
21
(C) If the candidate transmits the My 
22
Voice Voucher to the Commission, the Commis-
23
sion shall pay the candidate the portion of the 
24
value of the My Voice Voucher that the indi-
25
vidual allocated to the candidate, which shall be 
26
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
546 
•HR 1 IH
considered a contribution by the individual to 
1
the candidate for purposes of the Federal Elec-
2
tion Campaign Act of 1971. 
3
(2) DESIGNATION
OF
QUALIFIED
INDIVID-
4
UALS.—For purposes of paragraph (1)(A), a ‘‘quali-
5
fied individual’’ with respect to a State means an in-
6
dividual— 
7
(A) who is a resident of the State; 
8
(B) who will be of voting age as of the 
9
date of the election for the candidate to whom 
10
the individual submits a My Voice Voucher; and 
11
(C) who is not prohibited under Federal 
12
law from making contributions to candidates 
13
for election for Federal office. 
14
(3) TREATMENT
AS
CONTRIBUTION
TO
CAN-
15
DIDATE.—For purposes of the Federal Election 
16
Campaign Act of 1971, the submission of a My 
17
Voice Voucher to a candidate by an individual shall 
18
be treated as a contribution to the candidate by the 
19
individual in the amount of the portion of the value 
20
of the Voucher that the individual allocated to the 
21
candidate. 
22
(b) FRAUD PREVENTION MECHANISM.—In addition 
23
to the elements described in subsection (a), a State oper-
24
ating a voucher pilot program under this part shall permit 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
547 
•HR 1 IH
an individual to revoke a My Voice Voucher not later than 
1
2 days after submitting the My Voice Voucher to a can-
2
didate. 
3
(c) OVERSIGHT COMMISSION.—In addition to the ele-
4
ments described in subsection (a), a State operating a 
5
voucher pilot program under this part shall establish a 
6
commission or designate an existing entity to oversee and 
7
implement the program in the State, except that no such 
8
commission or entity may be comprised of elected officials. 
9
(d) PUBLIC INFORMATION CAMPAIGN.—In addition 
10
to the elements described in subsection (a), a State oper-
11
ating a voucher pilot program under this part shall carry 
12
out a public information campaign to disseminate aware-
13
ness of the program among qualified individuals. 
14
SEC. 5103. REPORTS. 
15
(a) PRELIMINARY
REPORT.—Not later than 6 
16
months after the first election cycle of the program oper-
17
ation period, a State which operates a voucher pilot pro-
18
gram under this part shall submit a report to the Commis-
19
sion analyzing the operation and effectiveness of the pro-
20
gram during the cycle and including such other informa-
21
tion as the Commission may require. 
22
(b) FINAL REPORT.—Not later than 6 months after 
23
the end of the program operation period, the State shall 
24
submit a final report to the Commission analyzing the op-
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
548 
•HR 1 IH
eration and effectiveness of the program and including 
1
such other information as the Commission may require. 
2
(c) REPORT BY COMMISSION.—Not later than the 
3
end of the first election cycle which begins after the pro-
4
gram operation period, the Commission shall submit a re-
5
port to Congress which summarizes and analyzes the re-
6
sults of the voucher pilot program, and shall include in 
7
the report such recommendations as the Commission con-
8
siders appropriate regarding the expansion of the pilot 
9
program to all States and territories, along with such 
10
other recommendations and other information as the Com-
11
mission considers appropriate. 
12
SEC. 5104. DEFINITIONS. 
13
(a) ELECTION CYCLE.—In this part, the term ‘‘elec-
14
tion cycle’’ means the period beginning on the day after 
15
the date of the most recent regularly scheduled general 
16
election for Federal office and ending on the date of the 
17
next regularly scheduled general election for Federal of-
18
fice. 
19
(b) DEFINITIONS RELATING TO PERIODS.—In this 
20
part, the following definitions apply: 
21
(1) PROGRAM APPLICATION PERIOD.—The term 
22
‘‘program application period’’ means the first elec-
23
tion cycle which begins after the date of the enact-
24
ment of this Act. 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
549 
•HR 1 IH
(2) PROGRAM
PREPARATION
PERIOD.—The 
1
term ‘‘program preparation period’’ means the first 
2
election cycle which begins after the program appli-
3
cation period. 
4
(3) PROGRAM OPERATION PERIOD.—The term 
5
‘‘program operation period’’ means the first 2 elec-
6
tion cycles which begin after the program prepara-
7
tion period.

‘‘SEC. 547. ELECTION CYCLE DEFINED. 
4
‘‘In this title, the term ‘election cycle’ means, with 
5
respect to an election for an office, the period beginning 
6

7

9

10
(or, if the general election resulted in a runoff election, 
11
the date of the runoff election).’’.

Subtitle D—Personal Use Services 
1
as Authorized Campaign Ex-
2
penditures 
3
SEC. 5301. SHORT TITLE; FINDINGS; PURPOSE. 
4
(a) SHORT TITLE.—This subtitle may be cited as the 
5
‘‘Help America Run Act’’. 
6
(b) FINDINGS.—Congress finds the following: 
7
(1) Everyday Americans experience barriers to 
8
entry before they can consider running for office to 
9
serve their communities. 
10
(2) Current law states that campaign funds 
11
cannot be spent on everyday expenses that would 
12
exist whether or not a candidate were running for 
13
office, like childcare and food. While the law seems 
14
neutral, its actual effect is to privilege the independ-
15
ently wealthy who want to run, because given the de-
16
mands of running for office, candidates who must 
17
work to pay for childcare or to afford health insur-
18
ance are effectively being left out of the process, 
19
even if they have sufficient support to mount a via-
20
ble campaign. 
21
(3) Thus current practice favors those prospec-
22
tive candidates who do not need to rely on a regular 
23
paycheck to make ends meet. The consequence is 
24
that everyday Americans who have firsthand knowl-
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
637 
•HR 1 IH
edge of the importance of stable childcare, a safety 
1
net, or great public schools are less likely to get a 
2
seat at the table. This governance by the few is anti-
3
thetical to the democratic experiment, but most im-
4
portantly, when lawmakers do not share the con-
5
cerns of everyday Americans, their policies reflect 
6
that. 
7
(4) These circumstances have contributed to a 
8
Congress that does not always reflect everyday 
9
Americans. The New York Times reported in 2019 
10
that fewer than 5 percent of representatives cite 
11
blue-collar or service jobs in their biographies. A 
12
2015 survey by the Center for Responsive Politics 
13
showed that the median net worth of lawmakers was 
14
just over $1 million in 2013, or 18 times the wealth 
15
of the typical American household. 
16
(5) These circumstances have also contributed 
17
to a governing body that does not reflect the nation 
18
it serves. For instance, women are 51 percent of the 
19
American population. Yet even with a record number 
20
of women serving in the One Hundred Sixteenth 
21
Congress, the Pew Research Center notes that more 
22
than three out of four Members of this Congress are 
23
male. The Center for American Women And Politics 
24
found that one third of women legislators surveyed 
25
18:47 Jan 12, 2021
H1
638 
•HR 1 IH
had been actively discouraged from running for of-
1
fice, often by political professionals. This type of dis-
2
couragement, combined with the prohibitions on 
3
using campaign funds for domestic needs like 
4
childcare, burdens that still fall disproportionately 
5
on American women, particularly disadvantages 
6
working mothers. These barriers may explain why 
7
only 10 women in history have given birth while 
8
serving in Congress, in spite of the prevalence of 
9
working parents in other professions. Yet working 
10
mothers and fathers are best positioned to create 
11
policy that reflects the lived experience of most 
12
Americans. 
13
(6) Working mothers, those caring for their el-
14
derly parents, and young professionals who rely on 
15
their jobs for health insurance should have the free-
16
dom to run to serve the people of the United States. 
17
Their networks and net worth are simply not the 
18
best indicators of their strength as prospective pub-
19
lic servants. In fact, helping ordinary Americans to 
20
run may create better policy for all Americans. 
21
(c) PURPOSE.—It is the purpose of this subtitle to 
22

23

24
their economic status. By expanding permissible uses of 
25

SEC. 8015. LEGAL DEFENSE FUNDS. 
8
(a) DEFINITIONS.—In this section— 
9

10

11

12

13
(A) that has only one beneficiary; 
14

15

16

17

18
the trust is established; 
19
(C) that is subject to a trust agreement 
20
that provides for the mandatory public disclo-
21
sure of all donations and disbursements; 
22
(D) that is subject to a trust agreement 
23
that prohibits the use of its resources for any 
24
purpose other than— 
25
